<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10191847074948738444">In this powerfully funny, razor's edge tale of a fractured girlhood, prize-winning poet and critic Mary Karr conjures up the terrors and joys of growing up in a swampy East Texas refinery town, at the epicenter of a family full of passionate, volatie attachments. In a voice stipped of self-pity, in language reinvented with a raw authenticity and brilliant energy, Karr show</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10191847074948738444">In this powerfully funny, razor's edge tale of a fractured girlhood, prize-winning poet and critic Mary Karr conjures up the terrors and joys of growing up in a swampy East Texas refinery town, at the epicenter of a family full of passionate, volatie attachments. In a voice stipped of self-pity, in language reinvented with a raw authenticity and brilliant energy, Karr shows readers a "terrific family of liars and drunks . . . redeemed by a slow unearthing of truth".</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10191847074948738444">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_78615934">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78615934" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/763271-nancy" class="left imgcol" title="Nancy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1435597527p2/763271.jpg" alt="Nancy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/78615934?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 24, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/763271-nancy" class="user" title="Nancy">Nancy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=bio-and-memoirs" class="actionLinkLite">bio-and-memoirs</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=library-books" class="actionLinkLite">library-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=shelf-inflicted" class="actionLinkLite">shelf-inflicted</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/763271-nancy?shelf=award-winners" class="actionLinkLite">award-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer78615934">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2470124953767588671">Posted at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2013/11/the-liars-club.html">Shelf Inflicted</a> <br /><br />After reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77630715">Will's</a> intriguing review of <a title="Lit by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6340016.Lit">Lit: A Memoir</a>, I decided it was time to explore Mary Karr’s work, so I went to the library and borrowed <a title="The Liars' Club by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14241.The_Liars__Club">The Liars' Club</a>. Written in 1995, this memoir explores the author’s dysfunctional childhood in sweltering and swampy Leechfield, Texas.<br /><br />Though Mary Karr and I did not have similar childhoods, there were definitely certain life situations and reactions to them that I could relate to and I came to realize that no matter how diffe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2470124953767588671">Posted at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2013/11/the-liars-club.html">Shelf Inflicted</a> <br /><br />After reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77630715">Will's</a> intriguing review of <a title="Lit by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6340016.Lit">Lit: A Memoir</a>, I decided it was time to explore Mary Karr’s work, so I went to the library and borrowed <a title="The Liars' Club by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14241.The_Liars__Club">The Liars' Club</a>. Written in 1995, this memoir explores the author’s dysfunctional childhood in sweltering and swampy Leechfield, Texas.<br /><br />Though Mary Karr and I did not have similar childhoods, there were definitely certain life situations and reactions to them that I could relate to and I came to realize that no matter how different people’s lives are, there are always things that will connect us. I was enthralled by her writing, which is very intimate and lyrical and enabled me to empathize with her. <br /><br />Just like my dad, Mary’s dad saved receipts from nearly every bill he paid (and he always voted Democrat).<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>“It was a feat Daddy never got to perform, but on nights when he spread the receipts out chronologically, he made it clear to my sister and me that every day some suit-wearing, Republican sonofabitch (his term) weaseled a working man out of an extra three dollars for lack of a receipt. He would not be caught short. These notorious Republicans were the bogeymen of my childhood. When I asked him to define one (I think it was during the Kennedy-Nixon debate), Daddy said a Republican was somebody who couldn’t enjoy eating unless he knew somebody else was hungry, which I took to be gospel for longer than I care to admit. Maybe the only thing worse than being a Republican was being a scab.”</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />My brother was an obedient child and always stayed still while my dad was smacking him. To me, that made no sense and just made it easy for my dad. If I put enough energy into evading and escaping, dad had to spend energy grabbing for my wrist or bending over to yank me out from under the bed. This did not exhaust him, as I hoped it would, but only made the beating worse when he finally got a hold of me. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>“Of course, I am famous for running in the middle of a spanking. It makes me proud that Daddy used to run too. I always figured only a dumbass would just stand still and take it.”</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />So Mary’s parents weren’t so great, but I loved the details she used to bring them to life. Even with all their flaws, I found it difficult to dislike them totally. My parents weren’t so perfect either. For the most part I was able to get past that when I remind myself they are human beings with flaws, and children don’t come with an instruction manual, and people just do the best they can. It’s not always good enough, but it’s easier to understand and empathize with your flawed parents when you’re not putting them up on a pedestal attributing superhuman qualities to them. <br /><br />This is a very worthwhile story about hardship, family relationships, survival, and growing up. It is absorbing, disturbing, hilarious, honest and difficult to put down. I am looking forward to reading about Mary’s teen years.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2470124953767588671">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/590988025" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/885274-bryan-furuness" class="left imgcol" title="Bryan Furuness"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1353528308p2/885274.jpg" alt="Bryan Furuness" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/590988025?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 15, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/885274-bryan-furuness" class="user" title="Bryan Furuness">Bryan Furuness</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/885274-bryan-furuness?shelf=first-person" class="actionLinkLite">first-person</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/885274-bryan-furuness?shelf=storytelling" class="actionLinkLite">storytelling</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/885274-bryan-furuness?shelf=memoir" class="actionLinkLite">memoir</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/885274-bryan-furuness?shelf=woman-writer" class="actionLinkLite">woman-writer</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/885274-bryan-furuness?shelf=2013" class="actionLinkLite">2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/885274-bryan-furuness?shelf=nonfic" class="actionLinkLite">nonfic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer590988025">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14722511740948986092">I don't write a lot of customer reviews. And when I finished this book, I didn't think it needed my review. For one thing, I'm probably the last person in the hemisphere to read it; for another, this book is so good and has been popular for so long that its ratings must be sky high, right? <br /><br />At the time that I'm writing this review, the Goodreads rating is 3.88. <br /><br />Over 2000 people gave it one or two stars. <br /><br />People, for real. What are you looking for in a book? Karr has given you a gem, a freaking gem</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14722511740948986092">I don't write a lot of customer reviews. And when I finished this book, I didn't think it needed my review. For one thing, I'm probably the last person in the hemisphere to read it; for another, this book is so good and has been popular for so long that its ratings must be sky high, right? <br /><br />At the time that I'm writing this review, the Goodreads rating is 3.88. <br /><br />Over 2000 people gave it one or two stars. <br /><br />People, for real. What are you looking for in a book? Karr has given you a gem, a freaking gem. She's got a poet's sense of the line, and a novelist's sense of structure. The sentences are the best that I've read in a year. Every one is sharp, by which I mean both smart and cutting. The way she bounces the reader through time seems to mimic the way memory works, only with much more clarity. Bad shit happens in this book, but she avoids pity and prurience, turning that shit into art. <br /><br />Five stars. Five. And if I had super hacker skills, I would revoke the ranking privileges of everyone who gave this book one star. If you don't like this book, you don't like books. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14722511740948986092">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_21529987">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21529987" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/306689-dorothea" class="left imgcol" title="Dorothea"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1439076667p2/306689.jpg" alt="Dorothea" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/21529987?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/306689-dorothea" class="user" title="Dorothea">Dorothea</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/306689-dorothea?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21529987">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11811781058314099283"><em>The Liars' Club</em> is Mary Karr's memoir of her childhood growing up in a small, east Texas oil town, and was first published in 1995. The thought of how this woman's writing has managed to escape me until two weeks ago is unnerving. I blame all of you, actually, for not telling me about her sooner. Jesus and the angels will help me recover from this most bitter betrayal.<br /><br />From the first page of this book I was sucked in. I had to sleep with it next to my head on my pillow and carry it around with me</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11811781058314099283"><em>The Liars' Club</em> is Mary Karr's memoir of her childhood growing up in a small, east Texas oil town, and was first published in 1995. The thought of how this woman's writing has managed to escape me until two weeks ago is unnerving. I blame all of you, actually, for not telling me about her sooner. Jesus and the angels will help me recover from this most bitter betrayal.<br /><br />From the first page of this book I was sucked in. I had to sleep with it next to my head on my pillow and carry it around with me at all times. When I finished it, I wanted to read again. Her writing is brutal, ballsy, alluring and sharp.<br /><br />Mary was the type of child who, at the age of nine, climbed up a tree and started shooting bbs at the family of a boy who insulted her. She would flip off her grade school teachers and tell other authority figures to, "Eat me raw," thinking it was just another way of saying, "Kiss my ass."<br /><br />Yeah, so when you see your neighbors' children behaving like anarchist strippers on crack and you wonder if you should call child-protective services, you might want to read this book first, because its hilarious and tragic and the best damn thing I've read in a long time. And I'm not saying it should have any bearing on you making that call. That's between you and God. What I'm saying is Brutally Sharp Alluring Balls, people, that's all I'm sayin'.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11811781058314099283">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_14805914">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14805914" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/173457-heather" class="left imgcol" title="Heather"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1377456181p2/173457.jpg" alt="Heather" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14805914?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 11, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/173457-heather" class="user" title="Heather">Heather</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Memoir fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/173457-heather?shelf=memoir" class="actionLinkLite">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14805914">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18008008260336337025">Much praise has been written about Mary Karr's uniqe poetic voice. But, honestly? I found very little that was "special" about Mary Karr. Her writing style seems jarring; she has no problem jumping around in time in the middle of a paragraph. I also found it difficult to be compelled with her story. It was a story about growing up poor in a industrial town in Texas. I bet 30 other kids from that same town could have written a very similar book. Her prose was bland and it was evident that she did</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18008008260336337025">Much praise has been written about Mary Karr's uniqe poetic voice. But, honestly? I found very little that was "special" about Mary Karr. Her writing style seems jarring; she has no problem jumping around in time in the middle of a paragraph. I also found it difficult to be compelled with her story. It was a story about growing up poor in a industrial town in Texas. I bet 30 other kids from that same town could have written a very similar book. Her prose was bland and it was evident that she didn't like her past self very much. It's very difficult to read about a character that the author feels embarassed about, whether it is fiction or non-fiction.<br /><br />I did enjoy a few of the more truthful-sounding snippets about her parents and her childhood thoughts about death and seperation. To me, however, the majority of the story fell flat.<br /><br />My biggest problem is that this "hard childhood" memoir just doesn't hold a light to The Glass Castle - the memoir that got me interested in reading memoirs again.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18008008260336337025">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_72155274">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72155274" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2765803-emily-green" class="left imgcol" title="Emily Green"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1313125586p2/2765803.jpg" alt="Emily Green" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/72155274?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 16, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2765803-emily-green" class="user" title="Emily Green">Emily Green</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer72155274">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2271973532722687008">I had heard a great deal about Mary Karr's _The Liars' Club_ before I read it. _The Liars' Club_ is considered one of the groundbreaking books in the current memoir movement, and there is much for a writer to learn from it, both things to steal and things to avoid.<br /><br />To steal, of course, are the humor and honesty. One of my favorite moments occurs when Karr explains that she and her sister misheard the phrase "It ain't the heat, it's the humidity" for years, believing people said, "It ain't the hea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2271973532722687008">I had heard a great deal about Mary Karr's _The Liars' Club_ before I read it. _The Liars' Club_ is considered one of the groundbreaking books in the current memoir movement, and there is much for a writer to learn from it, both things to steal and things to avoid.<br /><br />To steal, of course, are the humor and honesty. One of my favorite moments occurs when Karr explains that she and her sister misheard the phrase "It ain't the heat, it's the humidity" for years, believing people said, "It ain't the heat, it's the stupidity." Brilliant. And Karr is always forthright about both the perceptions of the child and the adult narrator. The descriptions of her parents spare no one involved, but in the end, do not condemn nor place their heads on stakes.<br /><br />Karr also organizes her book well--it becomes a story, not just the relating of her childhood. The people become characters, including herself. When we read, we often look for elements which we can relate to, and the inner workings of family, as well as how they differ from the outer workings of the world, are issues with which we all must struggle.<br /><br />Not to mention Karr has found herself a brilliant title.<br /><br />Now, while the book was enjoyable, for the most part, child abuse, especially sexual abuse, isn't a topic most people enjoy reading about. When Karr published _The Liars' Club,_ she was one of the first in the movement to reveal so graphically her sufferings. Perhaps _The Liars' Club_ can be viewed as a book that opened dialogue for survivors of sexual abuse, and probably did many a great service. However, the climate has changed a bit. With books like _Running with Scissors_ filling the shelves, discussion of abuse has become common, almost to the point of cliche. It's difficult to provide catharsis for the reader, almost as difficult as it is to provide healing for the self after such trauma. Abuse in a memoir cannot be merely revelation, it must be an integral part of the story that serves the plot as well as the development of the character/memoirist. It cannot be there just because it happened, just like any event in the book cannot be there just because it occurred in the writer's life. And the reader wants some catharsis, some acknowledgment of the memoir that the writer not only lived through the abuse, but was able to thrive. We don't want a victim. Harsh, maybe, but we readers don't just want a tell-all, we want a tell-all that also makes a good story.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2271973532722687008">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a href="/review/list/2624836-reese?shelf=memoirs" class="actionLinkLite">memoirs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer124473450">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4579838785363030497">NOTE: THE LIARS'CLUB four-star rating does not mean that I "really liked it."<br /><br />I usually love memoirs. (Well, not ones written by narcissists or liars.) If I were young enough to have read Mary Karr's THE LIARS' CLUB (1995) when I was in my early twenties, I might well have appreciated it to the extent that the work deserves. Alas, another if. Unfortunately, I've grown old, old enough to "wear my trousers rolled" (T.S. Eliot). And in the past year, this old person has read too much material (ficti</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4579838785363030497">NOTE: THE LIARS'CLUB four-star rating does not mean that I "really liked it."<br /><br />I usually love memoirs. (Well, not ones written by narcissists or liars.) If I were young enough to have read Mary Karr's THE LIARS' CLUB (1995) when I was in my early twenties, I might well have appreciated it to the extent that the work deserves. Alas, another if. Unfortunately, I've grown old, old enough to "wear my trousers rolled" (T.S. Eliot). And in the past year, this old person has read too much material (fiction and nonfiction) that presents in detail the awful, the ugly, the sickening, the infuriating experiences of children whose parents are not fit to be parents. With my zero-tolerance for the neglect and abuse of children, I found making my way through THE LIARS' CLUB a chore that I kept postponing.<br /><br />Yes, I could have put Karr's memoir back on a shelf or added it to my bookswap list after I had read fifty (or fewer) pages; but some unidentifiable voice, for some unknown reason, stopped me from abandoning it. Was I supposed to finish the book so that I would learn what most dimwits know: rotten parents don't necessarily create rotten children??? I've never accepted Huck Finn's assertion that "a body that don't get started [ital.] right when he's little ain't got no show." The memoir's last section, an account of events that occur seventeen years after the previous section, seems to be the author's way of (1)dealing with old issues and (2)declaring: "See -- good children can happen to bad parents." And if that is what Karr wants to say, my response is "What's your point?" No, I didn't miss the "point" -- I dismissed it. <br /><br />While I'm glad that I'm not STILL reading THE LIARS' CLUB, I am, nevertheless, glad that I HAVE READ it. It's a valuable book -- if for no other reason than its potential to get readers to notice that where blame lies is not what matters. Granted, we can explain and justify and excuse the behavior of irresponsible parents by looking at their parents and at the unfortunate circumstances that helped to produce each generation. But to what end? In a description of some of her mother's "crying jag[s]," Karr does reveal what really counts: "Then she would bawl like a sick cat, hanging her head in her hands. . . and saying that we didn't understand, and that it wasn't our fault that she was crying. Like we cared whose fault it was instead of just wanting it stopped"(132). The alcoholism, the violence, the lunacy, and so on. STOPPED. All else is not very useful commentary.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4579838785363030497">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/646385-nick" class="user" title="Nick">Nick</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer100135503">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3386508809622223170">I fully anticipated that I would love this book. Almost everyone else has. And has then gone on to love her two subsequent memoirs. But, I have to say, I probably found the 10th Anniversary Foreword and the last chapter (when the reader finds out, at least in part, why her mother is so insane) the most compelling. The rest of it I just couldn't get into. <br /><br />It's not that nothing happens -- because plenty does -- but at times I felt like SO MUCH happened that the reader wasn't given any clue as to w</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3386508809622223170">I fully anticipated that I would love this book. Almost everyone else has. And has then gone on to love her two subsequent memoirs. But, I have to say, I probably found the 10th Anniversary Foreword and the last chapter (when the reader finds out, at least in part, why her mother is so insane) the most compelling. The rest of it I just couldn't get into. <br /><br />It's not that nothing happens -- because plenty does -- but at times I felt like SO MUCH happened that the reader wasn't given any clue as to what was actually important and what was just detail. I didn't feel like there was any narrative arc. And I couldn't bring myself to care all that much, about the story or about the characters.<br /><br />Which brings me to my final point: the majority of the book covers Karr's childhood before the age of 8. I understand that memoir can be just as subjective as fiction and I'm not bothered by this. I also read that Karr thanks her mother and sister in the acknowledgments for helping her to recall certain events. But honestly, I find it seriously hard to believe that she could possibly have the memory that she claims to have. It's astonishing, particularly in the detail that she claims to recall (and there is a lot of detail; see above). <br /><br />The weird thing is that it's quite clear that Mary Karr can write, certainly at the level of the sentence and indeed of the paragraph. She constructs both beautifully and it's not hard to believe that she is a poet, which she is. But she was not a poet who made me all that interested in what, in theory at least, could have been a pretty interesting story. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3386508809622223170">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=biography-memoir" class="actionLinkLite">biography-memoir</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=around-the-usa" class="actionLinkLite">around-the-usa</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-usa-texas" class="actionLinkLite">location-usa-texas</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1411033918">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6906493210431177957">After reading Mary Karr's <a title="The Art of Memoir by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25508114.The_Art_of_Memoir">The Art of Memoir</a>, I decided to give her memoirs another try. Instead of starting with <a title="Lit by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6340016.Lit">Lit</a>, a book I had already abandoned, I decided to start with <a title="Cherry by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/217402.Cherry">Cherry</a> because it was 50 cents at a book sale. Just a few pages in it became clear that the author was continuing her story that she'd started in The Liars' Club, so I went back to the beginning.<br /><br />I think well-written memoir pulls you in by finding common ground. Was I surprised to find common ground with a child of east-Texas</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6906493210431177957">After reading Mary Karr's <a title="The Art of Memoir by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25508114.The_Art_of_Memoir">The Art of Memoir</a>, I decided to give her memoirs another try. Instead of starting with <a title="Lit by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6340016.Lit">Lit</a>, a book I had already abandoned, I decided to start with <a title="Cherry by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/217402.Cherry">Cherry</a> because it was 50 cents at a book sale. Just a few pages in it became clear that the author was continuing her story that she'd started in The Liars' Club, so I went back to the beginning.<br /><br />I think well-written memoir pulls you in by finding common ground. Was I surprised to find common ground with a child of east-Texas 1960s with an alcoholic mother? Well yes, a bit. But it was astonishing how much of it resonated with me. Apart from that Karr is an excellent writer and you can tell that she has agonized over some of the passages in this book. They aren't just a tall tale of a rough childhood, they are crafted sentences and turned phrases. I appreciated that aspect of it.<br /><br />About her relationship with her sister:<br />"(In fights Lecia and I have as grown-ups, she'll scream at me, 'You were always so fucking cute!' And I'll scream back, 'You were always so fucking incompetent!')"<br /><br />When her mother was in a mental facility:<br />"'I'm sorry you're all locked up,' I said, which made her laugh.<br />'Shit, honey,' she said, 'you-all are locked up, too. You're just in a bigger room.'"<br /><br />On her relationship with her father:<br />"So over the years, Daddy and I grew abstract to each other. We knew each other in theory and loved in theory. But if placed in proximity - when I came home, say - any room we sat in would eventually fall into a soul-sucking quiet I could hardly stand."<br /><br />Also discussed on <a rel="nofollow" href="http://readingenvy.blogspot.com/2015/11/reading-envy-044-of-survival-and-memory.html">Episode 044</a> of the Reading Envy Podcast, along with some discussion of <a title="The Art of Memoir by Mary Karr" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25508114.The_Art_of_Memoir">The Art of Memoir</a>.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_376121941">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/376121941" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl" class="left imgcol" title="Cheryl"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442870534p2/11255849.jpg" alt="Cheryl" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/376121941?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 16, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl" class="user" title="Cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=memoirs" class="actionLinkLite">memoirs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer376121941">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6347034674664265260">A woman covers up a secret younger life when she marries (or remarries) and has two children. Yet the trauma of that life follows her. The result is this memoir from one of her daughters whose Texas and Colorado childhood is laced with remnants of her mother's former life and other self--because her mother seems to be two people. You know, the choices-of-the-mother-affects-the-daughter thing. This is a young girl's story of living with parents who suffer from mental illness and alcoholism. I wou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6347034674664265260">A woman covers up a secret younger life when she marries (or remarries) and has two children. Yet the trauma of that life follows her. The result is this memoir from one of her daughters whose Texas and Colorado childhood is laced with remnants of her mother's former life and other self--because her mother seems to be two people. You know, the choices-of-the-mother-affects-the-daughter thing. This is a young girl's story of living with parents who suffer from mental illness and alcoholism. I would add PTSD perhaps (her dad being a veteran who was also wounded twice). Melancholic and courageous story-telling told with retrospective ease. The type of "voice" that seeks no pity, rather choosing to educate through life experiences. <br /><br />Like this pivotal moment (I won't spoil it by saying what happened before) when she stops caring about what people think: "For the first time, I felt the power my family's strangeness gave us over the neighbors. Those other grown-ups were scared. Not only of my parents but of me. My wildness scared them. Plus they guessed that I'd moved through houses darker than theirs. All my life I'd wanted to belong in their families, to draw my lunch bag from the simple light and order of their defrosted refrigerators. The stories that got whispered behind our supermarket cart, or the silence that fell over the credit union when Daddy shoved open the glass door--these things always set my face burning. That afternoon, for the first time, I believed that Death itself lived in the neighboring houses. Death cheered for the Dallas Cowboys, and wrapped canned biscuit dough around Vienna sausages for the half-time snack." <br /><br />The second part of the book is when the words really jumped off the page for me though. I'm now curious, and will be looking into "Cherry" and "Lit" from Mary Karr. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6347034674664265260">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_6625289">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6625289" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/406535-adam" class="left imgcol" title="Adam"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1190514738p2/406535.jpg" alt="Adam" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/6625289?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 24, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/406535-adam" class="user" title="Adam">Adam</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/406535-adam?shelf=adamsfavoritebooks" class="actionLinkLite">adamsfavoritebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer6625289">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1192439440512466642">This memoir covers Mary Karr's childhood years to about her teens (with some later teen/early 20s at the end). I've read her other books and not been as impressed, but "The Liar's Club" is great writing about growing up in a strange family in an East Texas oil town, in the 60s/70s. Her dad is an oil field worker who is a great, loving father, but with a drinking problem, violent streaks, and her mother is an artist with clear mental health problems who doesn't fit in a little town in East Texas.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1192439440512466642">This memoir covers Mary Karr's childhood years to about her teens (with some later teen/early 20s at the end). I've read her other books and not been as impressed, but "The Liar's Club" is great writing about growing up in a strange family in an East Texas oil town, in the 60s/70s. Her dad is an oil field worker who is a great, loving father, but with a drinking problem, violent streaks, and her mother is an artist with clear mental health problems who doesn't fit in a little town in East Texas. <br />I like it for three main reasons. First, she's excellent at describing the setting; the background of a hot, gritty, smelly East Texas town fills the story, as does their beach escapades and dalliance to Colorado. Second, part of the premise (indicated by the title) is that storytellers have a right if not obligation to make the story a bit bigger than reality would suggest - so the reader is never sure what is truth and what is bigger than truth. <br />Most importantly, her own character is just incredible. Spunky and mean and tough, she goes through a lot of horrible things, sees and experiences things a child shouldn't, but really fights back. Your heart breaks a few times but I think by the end of it you realize how powerful children can be. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1192439440512466642">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1020845112">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1020845112" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5819312-sheryl-sorrentino" class="left imgcol" title="Sheryl Sorrentino"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442599791p2/5819312.jpg" alt="Sheryl Sorrentino" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1020845112?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 01, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5819312-sheryl-sorrentino" class="user" title="Sheryl Sorrentino">Sheryl Sorrentino</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1020845112">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15908380338093779442">Like many of the low-star reviewers, I really wanted to love this book because it was recommended to me by a friend and colleague. But it did not hold my interest and I found myself not especially wanting to return to it. I kept at it, though, because I expected it to improve and wanted to have the complete picture before rendering judgment. In the end, for me it fell flat.<br /><br />The writing, while sometimes clever and often humorous, utterly lacked any richness of emotion. I think that is why I could</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15908380338093779442">Like many of the low-star reviewers, I really wanted to love this book because it was recommended to me by a friend and colleague. But it did not hold my interest and I found myself not especially wanting to return to it. I kept at it, though, because I expected it to improve and wanted to have the complete picture before rendering judgment. In the end, for me it fell flat.<br /><br />The writing, while sometimes clever and often humorous, utterly lacked any richness of emotion. I think that is why I could not get engaged, despite the sometimes attention-grabbing goings-on. The author cloaks some rather horrific events with an opaque mask of what I believe to be desensitization. For example, her mother supposedly shot her boyfriend, Hector, but in the next scene, he is perfectly fine and we are back to shenanigans. The mother nearly drove her two kids purposefully off a bridge escaping a tornado, and yet, other than the author’s internal confusion in doubting what had just happened, they arrived at their destination moments in front of the storm no worse for wear. Did these things actually happen that way, or were they supposed to be the product of an overactive childhood imagination? This was not entirely clear to me, and I think in order for such scenes to pack punch, they have to be described in such a way that the reader can connect with why it mattered enough to the protagonist (in this case, the author) to tell about it.<br /><br />In a similar vein, the terror the author felt for her grandmother was somehow not relatable, despite the fact that Karr presents her as a frightful character and the climax at the end (regarding her prosthetic leg) was rather jolting. Again, despite good writing, some indefinable element was missing that caused the entire thing to fall flat.<br /><br />I agree with several reviewers that these parents, while quirky, unstable, and substance-addicted, were not abusive <em>per se,</em> but were like many parents: well-intentioned, loving, and often genuinely endearing, but addled by their own issues. In this regard, I saw many similarities with Jeannette Walls’ <em>The Glass Castle,</em> and must agree that is by far a superior book in this genre. <br /><br />I found the neat little “wrap-up” at the end to be a rather rushed and academic recitation of events meant to tie the whole lukewarm muddle together and justify that the mother was genuinely crazy. Presented that way, it fizzled even while providing legitimate answers. By that point, the answers were almost superfluous. I think this explanation could have been handled much better if it hadn’t been tagged on like an afterthought.<br /><br />Finally, I agree with other reviewers that many of the events recounted in this book were tedious and extraneous, and made it boring in places. That said, there are two graphic scenes that stand out, those being the instances of childhood sexual abuse. Though well-written, the first was blighted by the author’s real-time rant against her perpetrator. Don’t get me wrong, Karr is more than justified in harboring feelings of anger and resentment, but her memoir is not an appropriate public forum for such a personal censure. The second scene, though less inherently violent, is the more unsettling of the two because of the creepy, twisted, and languidly realistic (yet bizarrely artistic) depiction of this horrendous event. Though I almost couldn’t go on reading, because of the powerful realism and captivating writing, I must sourly elevate this book from three to three-and-a-half stars, even if that blasted emotional component remained stubbornly absent. Perhaps that is simply what happens when a child suffers this type of abuse at the hands of an adult. (As an aside, there was no explanation as to why the author’s mother left her sick eight-year-old daughter alone with a grown male babysitter, or who this man was. Was he a trusted neighbor or relative, or just some guy off the street? That absence of detail infuriated me almost as much as the scene itself.) <br /><br />On a personal note, I generally don’t post negative reviews, whether I am in the minority or the majority. I know firsthand how upsetting it feels to be on the receiving end of them, and have often asked myself why readers would feel compelled to post harsh criticisms unless they’re purely peeved. (In those instances when I don’t like a book, I mark it “read” with no rating and leave it at that.) I therefore hope I will not get “trashed” for voicing a minority view on this one; I do so only to explain my three-star rating and with the stipulation that these are just my own opinions.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15908380338093779442">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_243567023">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/243567023" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/234985-ellen" class="left imgcol" title="Ellen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442603742p2/234985.jpg" alt="Ellen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/243567023?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 26, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/234985-ellen" class="user" title="Ellen">Ellen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer243567023">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13957005368302082968">So that's how you end a memoir. Case closed.<br /><br />I can't imagine the restraint and discipline required to write this book. Karr doesn't really tell you a damn thing. She lets the questions accrue, and you go along for the ride as they spool. There are so many questions (Wait, what the?) that you forget about half of them. And she never mentions any of them explicitly anyway, as in "I always wondered about..." Nope. She doesn't really explain. You're just in this fog of incident and chronology, the sh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13957005368302082968">So that's how you end a memoir. Case closed.<br /><br />I can't imagine the restraint and discipline required to write this book. Karr doesn't really tell you a damn thing. She lets the questions accrue, and you go along for the ride as they spool. There are so many questions (Wait, what the?) that you forget about half of them. And she never mentions any of them explicitly anyway, as in "I always wondered about..." Nope. She doesn't really explain. You're just in this fog of incident and chronology, the shallow world of immediacy that is a child's experience not contextualized by history or understanding. But it's not foggy, exactly, because the moments and details are so vividly described. It's that overarching exposition that isn't there. <br /><br />At the end, little reminders start popping back up to the surface, and you go, "Oh yeah! I forgot about that." And the crazy thing is: some of the things were only a paragraph here or a sentence there, but you remember them because of however she wrote about it the first time around. She must have done it in a way that she planted it in your head somehow, and it's a surprise to find out that those things are still there at the end, lodged in your brain, that they must have been in some recess of your memory all along. It pops back and you get that moment of recognition. <br /><br />In the end, you get answers but you also get the questions, which were never really articulated by the author throughout the story. What a remarkable thing to save the questions, even, for the end. <br /><br />I laughed at this book more than I should have. It surprised me how funny it was. I wish there was a rating of 4.5 stars, but I save 5 stars for books that I could read many times again. Though I loved this, I'm not sure I would read it again.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13957005368302082968">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_413670913">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/413670913" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9009459-chris" class="left imgcol" title="Chris"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1335856829p2/9009459.jpg" alt="Chris" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/413670913?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 04, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9009459-chris" class="user" title="Chris">Chris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer413670913">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1443095084682414406">The first sentence in Stephen King's "On Writing" praises Mary Karr's "The Liars Club" as an example of excellent writing. So I thought: recommendation from a good source. It is a painful coming-of-age autobiographical narrative written from the adult author's point of view. Impossible as it may seem, it is told with look-back wisdom, love, and hard humor. Karr is an excellent writer. I thoroughly enjoyed the book and wholeheartedly recommend it. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_56964034">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56964034" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2343573-angie" class="left imgcol" title="Angie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442547765p2/2343573.jpg" alt="Angie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/56964034?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 21, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2343573-angie" class="user" title="Angie">Angie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2343573-angie?shelf=university" class="actionLinkLite">university</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2343573-angie?shelf=2007" class="actionLinkLite">2007</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2343573-angie?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer56964034'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer56964034">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16280833770053986621">This read like a Lifetime movie. Only instead of “based on a true story,” it actually is a true story.<br /><br />And it’s supposed to be funny. I really didn’t see any humor in it a lot. I even have a dark sense of humor, but sorry, reading about how the author gets raped twice before the age of 10 and her mother goes crazy and scribbles out her face in lipstick and sets a huge gasoline fire in the backyard and burns all of her paintings and her two children’s clothing and toys and all the violence that wa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16280833770053986621">This read like a Lifetime movie. Only instead of “based on a true story,” it actually is a true story.<br /><br />And it’s supposed to be funny. I really didn’t see any humor in it a lot. I even have a dark sense of humor, but sorry, reading about how the author gets raped twice before the age of 10 and her mother goes crazy and scribbles out her face in lipstick and sets a huge gasoline fire in the backyard and burns all of her paintings and her two children’s clothing and toys and all the violence that was going on and the fact that these two little girls basically stayed with their alcoholic mother so she “wouldn’t get into more trouble”... that’s funny?<br /><br />I think the author could have written the book better; it’s focused on her father, seeing as how he belonged to the Liar’s Club and it ends with his death, but most of the story involved the mother directly, and a lot seems to be left out. I don’t know what to think about this. It sounds like something you’d be better off telling your therapist.<br /><br />Reading the low-star amazon reviews makes me happy. I’m not the only one who didn’t like this and questioned the verisimilitude of many of her statements.<br /><br />This might have been the worst book I read this year.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16280833770053986621">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_105078618">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105078618" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1262421-richard" class="left imgcol" title="Richard"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1214250450p2/1262421.jpg" alt="Richard" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/105078618?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 16, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1262421-richard" class="user" title="Richard">Richard</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer105078618">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16059399895241966563">This is the first of three memoirs that make up her life in the small refinery town of Leechfield (nee Groves) Texas. "The Liar's Club" is a reference to Karr's father a bigger than life character who spent his adult life working for Gulf or some other refinery in this backwater east Texas town (best known as the place where Agent Orange was produced). The books name derives from her father's penchant for drinking and telling famously big tales to his friends Cooter et al who relish his role as</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16059399895241966563">This is the first of three memoirs that make up her life in the small refinery town of Leechfield (nee Groves) Texas. "The Liar's Club" is a reference to Karr's father a bigger than life character who spent his adult life working for Gulf or some other refinery in this backwater east Texas town (best known as the place where Agent Orange was produced). The books name derives from her father's penchant for drinking and telling famously big tales to his friends Cooter et al who relish his role as the head liar. Fortunately for us, unlike her slightly older sister Lecia, Mary was dragged along to many of these bullshit sessions where she learned the to tell stories and to develop an edge that's ranges from sweet to lethal through the remainder of the book which culminates in her father’s death in her late teens, early 20's. <br /><br />Like any good memoir she tells the story of her life with unstinting honesty which is by turns horrible (her mother’s alcoholism, mental instability, affairs) and hysterically funny - a cute young girl who could cuss like a sailor, who carried her own BB gun and perched in a tree to exact vengeance on the neighbor kids for mocking her when her mother was removed to the mental institution. She tells her story with a bravery and beauty that is fearsome, revealing stories about being violated at an early age by a Neanderthal neighbor only to put a curse on him from the distance that only years and maturity can offer. Some of the passages are so funny that I realized I haven't laughed out loud while reading a book since I first read "Catcher In The Rye" at 17 in study hall.<br /><br />Perhaps best known as a poet ("Abacus", "Viper's Rum" etc., she is a professor of poetry and the memoir at Syracuse University. You'll never forget Mary Karr once you read The Liar's Club. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16059399895241966563">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_17388897">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17388897" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/541807-liz" class="left imgcol" title="Liz"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1193627638p2/541807.jpg" alt="Liz" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/17388897?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 24, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/541807-liz" class="user" title="Liz">Liz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone - there's a time in everyone's life when it's useful, I'm sure</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer17388897'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17388897">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10543268697106067273">(3/24/08): I've decided that, perhaps, reading books on which the header on the back cover says, "A Wickedly Funny Account of An Apocalyptic Childhood" are, maybe, not really helpful for me these days. <br /><br />Yes, the book was funny - in the beginning - despite the pain and the drama and the despair and the shock of the lengths that this author and her sister went to <em>to survive</em>. This dysfunctional family shows me how completely un-dysfunctional mine can be, when compared to them (which is, in itself, q</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10543268697106067273">(3/24/08): I've decided that, perhaps, reading books on which the header on the back cover says, "A Wickedly Funny Account of An Apocalyptic Childhood" are, maybe, not really helpful for me these days. <br /><br />Yes, the book was funny - in the beginning - despite the pain and the drama and the despair and the shock of the lengths that this author and her sister went to <em>to survive</em>. This dysfunctional family shows me how completely un-dysfunctional mine can be, when compared to them (which is, in itself, quite laughable). <br /><br />It is, at times, quite difficult to read. I sometimes like to live in a "pink and blue thoughts" world where the things that happened to this family <em> just don't happen</em>. I realize at the same time, the denial and refusal to discuss such only propagates that evil's strength in the world.<br /><br />There's no real resolution to this book; can there be in lives that aren't finished yet? It ends with a conversation that is so obvious to the reader that needs to have happened <em>years</em> earlier.<br /><br />It is a wonder to me that this author is (a) not institutionalized and (b) <strong>so very strong</strong>. I can see why the author mentions that so many people have been given this book to read by their therapists (FTR: mine didn't).<br /><br />Odd, isn't it, that such crap can just make you that much of a better person? At least I'm racking up good stories to tell . . .<br /><br />(3/9/08): Two quotes that stick out from the minute I've picked up this book in the introduction:<br /><em>"As certain facts that had once scalded all our insides and almost decimated our clan got broadcast a thousand times, we got oddly used to them. Call it aversion therapy, but the events seeped in a little deeper. We healed more - though that had never been the point - through exposure. Our distant catastrophes became somehow manageable. Catharsis, the Greeks call it."</em><br /><br />And this:<br /><em>"This is a writer's dream response, what I'd hankered for as a kid setting crayon to cardboard on Mother's Day - to plug a reader into some wall outlet deep in the personal psychic machine that might jumpstart him or her into a more feeling way of life."</em><br /><br />On page 30, but so far, it's riveting.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10543268697106067273">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_82038187">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82038187" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2320615-linden" class="left imgcol" title="Linden"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1262105925p2/2320615.jpg" alt="Linden" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/82038187?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 25, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2320615-linden" class="user" title="Linden">Linden</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer82038187">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7011354175893474998">This book has compelling images and moves along quickly. After reading about half of it, though, I realized that I was really irritated by the voice. She doesn't have much grace, and the wisdom she professes to have doesn't ring true. I started to feel very manipulated. She has plenty of painful memories, and she writes about them with a lot of sensory detail. But I didn't come away with a sense that she had made peace with her past, nor that she had a greater understanding of what life was all</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7011354175893474998">This book has compelling images and moves along quickly. After reading about half of it, though, I realized that I was really irritated by the voice. She doesn't have much grace, and the wisdom she professes to have doesn't ring true. I started to feel very manipulated. She has plenty of painful memories, and she writes about them with a lot of sensory detail. But I didn't come away with a sense that she had made peace with her past, nor that she had a greater understanding of what life was all about. It was more like "I have had this horrible stuff happen, look at me, look at me!" <br /><br />Then I heard her talk and realized that she is very, very angry and needs a lot of attention. She opened a poetry reading in Berkeley by cursing at the introducer and saying he was worthless. This totally sealed my opinion of her, which is that she hasn't gotten to a higher level of grace or wisdom but is selling her story because it sells. Period.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7011354175893474998">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_16995167">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16995167" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/592708-oceana9" class="left imgcol" title="Oceana9"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1193928719p2/592708.jpg" alt="Oceana9" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16995167?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/592708-oceana9" class="user" title="Oceana9">Oceana9</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Memoir buffs</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16995167">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10968298241867722682">A book from the "memoir craze," but really compelling in its starkness, and spare, truthful writing. A glimpse into what it's like to grow up in a sickening, ugly East Texas town (actually called Leechfield) with a crazy, alcoholic mother and an oil-drillin' father. Not schmaltzy or self-pitying for one second. My favorite scene is where the mother decides she doesn't want to be a "fucking hausfrau" anymore and proceeds to paint all the mirrors in the house with lipstick, cover her own face with</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10968298241867722682">A book from the "memoir craze," but really compelling in its starkness, and spare, truthful writing. A glimpse into what it's like to grow up in a sickening, ugly East Texas town (actually called Leechfield) with a crazy, alcoholic mother and an oil-drillin' father. Not schmaltzy or self-pitying for one second. My favorite scene is where the mother decides she doesn't want to be a "fucking hausfrau" anymore and proceeds to paint all the mirrors in the house with lipstick, cover her own face with lipstick, and start a bonfire in which she burns everything, I mean everything, in the house. I also like the way the mother will make split second decisions to move the whole family hundreds of miles away, and actually do it. The writing and subject matter reminded me of both Dorothy Allison and Tobias Wolff. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10968298241867722682">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer659149388130645530">I liked Karr's Texan twang and breezy story telling, even when it was so mismatched with the traumas she experienced. But what I felt was missing was some introspection from the viewpoint of the grownup Karr writing the story. I suppose her choice to narrate from the perspective of herself as a child was a way to help the reader get into her mind as she experienced it then - which she does by switching the story to a first person present tense. But I wanted to know what the Karr of today makes o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText659149388130645530">I liked Karr's Texan twang and breezy story telling, even when it was so mismatched with the traumas she experienced. But what I felt was missing was some introspection from the viewpoint of the grownup Karr writing the story. I suppose her choice to narrate from the perspective of herself as a child was a way to help the reader get into her mind as she experienced it then - which she does by switching the story to a first person present tense. But I wanted to know what the Karr of today makes of her parents, and of herself. I guess I wanted more than a portrayal of a dysfunctional childhood, - I wanted a "so what?" reflection of it as well. </span>
