<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_49659574">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49659574" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1333177-ciara" class="left imgcol" title="Ciara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442487190p2/1333177.jpg" alt="Ciara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/49659574?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 22, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1333177-ciara" class="user" title="Ciara">Ciara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people from oil towns, people dealing with fucked up families, aspiring memoirists</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1333177-ciara?shelf=autobio-memoir" class="actionLinkLite">autobio-memoir</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1333177-ciara?shelf=feminist-y-books" class="actionLinkLite">feminist-y-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1333177-ciara?shelf=read-in-2009" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer49659574">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7554626754941553994">stephen king raves about this book in his book about writing, <u>on writing</u>. i loved <u>on writing</u> &amp; think stephen king is an awesomely underrated craftsperson when it comes to writing, so i eventually got my act together &amp; checked this book out of the library. kind of astonishing that it took me like six years to do it! i read the whole thing in two days. it really is amazing! it's a memoir of mary karr's early years, growing up with a mental unstable mother &amp; an oil worker father who att</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7554626754941553994">stephen king raves about this book in his book about writing, <u>on writing</u>. i loved <u>on writing</u> &amp; think stephen king is an awesomely underrated craftsperson when it comes to writing, so i eventually got my act together &amp; checked this book out of the library. kind of astonishing that it took me like six years to do it! i read the whole thing in two days. it really is amazing! it's a memoir of mary karr's early years, growing up with a mental unstable mother &amp; an oil worker father who attempts to keep home life running peacefully for mary &amp; her older sister, lecia. the book seems to center on a few years, betweeh the ages of five &amp; nine or so, while mary's maternal grandmother moved in to wait to die of cancer, a neighbor boy molested mary, mary's mother lost her mind &amp; had herself committed to a mental institution, the family uprooted from west texas &amp; re-located to coloarado, mary's parents divorced, a man hired to babysit mary molested her, mary's mother re-married a local barfly &amp; proceeded to menace him with a gun, lecia convinced the girls' father to bring them back to texas, &amp; mary's mother returns to texas, divorces her barfly husband, &amp; re-marries the girls' father. the titular liars' club refers to the gathering of men that mary's father hung around with at the VA hall, playing cards &amp; drinking whiskey. they amused one another with long-winded reminisces of their youths, which were often complicated lies. mary's father was especially good at keeping the other men enthralled to his untruths. but the title also refers to the way memory makes liars of us all, with its convenient collusions that help us survive trauma &amp; grow up, even when the deck is stacked against us. <br /><br />the last few chapters recount mary's father's slow death following a stroke, &amp; mary's reconnections with her mom, questioning her about her secretive &amp; dramatic past, filled with ex-husbands &amp; secret children. <br /><br />i can't believe that somehow no one else ever recommended this book to me! it was right up my alley. i did think it was weird that all the reviews went on &amp; on about how how horiffic mary's childhood was, how unsettled &amp; traumatic. i didn't get that mary karr was <em>trying</em> to write a book about her unsettled, traumatic, horrifying childhood, so much as she was acknowledging that she'd crossed paths with some characters in her day, &amp; that they might make a good book. the sexual abuse is portrayed more as incidental than part of a narrative structure, although she points out that sexual abuse is a common narrative path winding through the lives of children growing up girl. certainly mary &amp; her sister survived some traumatic incidents, &amp; mary writes about dissociation &amp; learning to block memories out. but she also makes the point that all these cookie cutter happy-seeming families that she hoped would adopt her as a child come with their own baggage &amp; their own traumas, &amp; that her book wound up speaking to a more universal truth than she ever intended (i read the tenth anniversary re-issue, which comes with an introduction from the author on how the book has been received over the years). <br /><br />this book, especially all the well-written descriptions of oil tanks &amp; her dad coming home smelling like crude oil, brought up a lot of memories i thought i'd forgotten about my own family (my dad worked at an oil refinery). &amp; it made me think a lot about the way people recoil from some of my family stories, &amp; how, i guess, every unhappy family is unhappy in similar ways. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7554626754941553994">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8599250622913740074">This was pretty good. Not as good as The Glass Castle, but that was incredible. A lot of critics call Mary Karr's voice "poetic". Hmmm. Maybe, I guess. But to me, "poetic" is not a good thing. I do NOT want a lot of flowery language and metaphors and similies and all that. I just want the scoop told in a likable voice that resonnates (Jeannette Wall, props to that). But I didn't think the "poetic"-ness was too bad. When the whole story finally plays out, it's really heartbreaking. I was literall</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8599250622913740074">This was pretty good. Not as good as The Glass Castle, but that was incredible. A lot of critics call Mary Karr's voice "poetic". Hmmm. Maybe, I guess. But to me, "poetic" is not a good thing. I do NOT want a lot of flowery language and metaphors and similies and all that. I just want the scoop told in a likable voice that resonnates (Jeannette Wall, props to that). But I didn't think the "poetic"-ness was too bad. When the whole story finally plays out, it's really heartbreaking. I was literally gripping my heart through those 1o or 15 pages. How tragic. A lot of critics call this one funny, too. Hmmm. Nope, not so much for me. Except for the part where the little Mary Marlene is hanging from the window and truck drives by. That was REALLY funny. The whole naked family stuff was pretty funny, and a couple other parts too. But the overall tone of the book was not funny, no, not at all. If you just loved The Glass Castle and wish you had more, more, more, this would be a good one to pick up at the lib. Don't shell out the cash though. My library had 3 copies.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_34808259">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34808259" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/647547-mariel" class="left imgcol" title="Mariel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" alt="Mariel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/34808259?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 04, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/647547-mariel" class="user" title="Mariel">Mariel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">mommy dearests</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      my ex
  </div>



    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer34808259">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12503125555594522643">I read The Liar's Club when I was lonely and alone during a time I wasn't (in theory) supposed to be that way. Was it good company? Not really. I took away more than anything else from this book the feeling of trying to force acceptance of something that is unacceptable. I mean as a work of writing, as I really can't speak for Karr's heart and soul to what she feels and believes always (changing one's mind does happen, or so I'm told).<br /><br />There's a good way to write a memoir about super fucked up ti</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12503125555594522643">I read The Liar's Club when I was lonely and alone during a time I wasn't (in theory) supposed to be that way. Was it good company? Not really. I took away more than anything else from this book the feeling of trying to force acceptance of something that is unacceptable. I mean as a work of writing, as I really can't speak for Karr's heart and soul to what she feels and believes always (changing one's mind does happen, or so I'm told).<br /><br />There's a good way to write a memoir about super fucked up times and living situations and complicated people therein: don't try to rationalize pretty sick, pretty petty and selfish behavior with an in-short summation of how they aren't that bad after all if you didn't really earn that conclusion. I totally get being confused about it. You do want to like them if you ever idolized something about them, such as being glamorous like Karr's mom was (not something I ever idolized. I did admire creeps with great senses of humor, though). Yeah, evil to you moms who are easy with strangers (until they get past the stranger phase) would make great gay icons in the vein of Joan Crawford. Someone would get a kick out of it, sure, but sometimes you really, really need more than that. People are complicated: you don't need to whitewash over anything with an in-short anything. One relationship doesn't define another one. What matters is being there when you really needed them and it doesn't sound like her family were at all. Tell us what they meant to you and trust us to be able to work that other stuff out for ourselves. That's why she had such a fucked up life to write a memoir about it: it happened to her. Being funny and great looking in alligator boots (poor gators. they are ill treated in this book indeed) hardly matters beyond that? So yeah, the attempted understandings felt fake to me in the storytelling way. About papa, too. He failed her. If she remembers the babyhood feeling of safety, great, but c'mon you can have mixed feelings about it all. This is like being... lied to? (Shut up, Mariel. No one is laughing.) A therapist would say shut up and tell us how you really feel. The rest of this is bullshit to hide the fact. <br /><br />I was saddened by the sis growing up without her. That was my favorite part, how big sis joined adults sooner and left Karr behind to waddle in everlasting teen adolescence by her lonesome (know how that goes). I felt that was understanding how that happened and still admitting it was lonely. The whole book should have been like that.<br /><br />I needed more than that if I'm gonna take the time to get to know someone by reading their memoir. Funny, not funny enough to over come the rest.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12503125555594522643">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_487303915">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/487303915" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/54697-matt" class="left imgcol" title="matt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1353271036p2/54697.jpg" alt="matt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/487303915?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 22, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/54697-matt" class="user" title="matt">matt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=america-f-k-yeah" class="actionLinkLite">america-f-k-yeah</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=biography" class="actionLinkLite">biography</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=ladies-night" class="actionLinkLite">ladies-night</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer487303915">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9515401240794059296"><br />Idly picked up and it just took me over...I'm actually about halfway through now and Karr is just hitting every note, some blue and some grace notes, but one gets the feeling she's on the solo of her life...<br /><br />...and she didn't falter as the narrative went from bad to worse. I mean, it probably wasn't peaches and cream starting out in broke, dusty, barren nowheresville Texas and surrounded by sweat, bleakness and oil fields. <br /><br /> But you throw in some "low food stability" and some angry, drunken, fiery</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9515401240794059296"><br />Idly picked up and it just took me over...I'm actually about halfway through now and Karr is just hitting every note, some blue and some grace notes, but one gets the feeling she's on the solo of her life...<br /><br />...and she didn't falter as the narrative went from bad to worse. I mean, it probably wasn't peaches and cream starting out in broke, dusty, barren nowheresville Texas and surrounded by sweat, bleakness and oil fields. <br /><br /> But you throw in some "low food stability" and some angry, drunken, fiery-to-the-point-of-violence parents, the occasional sexual assault and rape and the constant wandering from place to place...it's pretty harrowing and pretty spectacular that she made it out and lived to tell the tale in a lucid, unsparing, witty and tough-mindedly poetic way.<br /><br /> Check this scene out:<br /><br /> "My father comes into focus for me on a Liar's Club afternoon. He sits at a wobbly card table weighed down by a bottle. Even now the scene seems so real to me that I can't but write it in the present tense.<br /><br /> I am dangling my legs off the bar at the Legion and shelling unroasted peanuts from a burlap bag while Daddy slides the domino tiles around the table. They make a clicking sound. I haven't started going to school yet, so the day seems without beginning or end, stalled in the beer-smelling dark of the Legion.<br /><br /> Cooter has just asked Daddy if he had planned to run away from home. "They wasn't no planning to it," Daddy says, then lights a cigarette to stall, picking a few strands of tobacco off his tongue as if that gesture may take all the time in the world. "Poppa had give me a silver dollar and told me to get into town and buy some coffee. Had to cross the train tracks to get there. When that old train come around the turn, it had to slow up. Well, when it slowed up, I jumped, and that dollar come with me.<br /><br /> "Got a job threshing wheat up to Kansas. Slept at night with some other old boys in this fella's barn. Man by the name of Hamlet. Sorriest sonofabitch ever to tread shoe leather. Wouldn't bring you a drink from sunup to lunch. And married to the prettiest woman you ever seen. A butt like two bulldogs in a sack." This makes everybody laugh.<br /><br /> I ask him how he got home, and he slides the story back on track. While I'm waiting for his answer, I split open a fresh peanut with my fingernail. The unroasted shell is soft as skin, the meat of the nut chewy and almost tasteless without salt. Daddy finishes his drink and moves a domino. "About didn't make it. Hopped the Double-E train from Kansas City to New Orleans. Cold"? He glares at each of us as if we might doubt the cold. "That wind come inching in those boxcar cracks like a straight razor. It'll cut your gizzard out, don't think it won't. They finally loaded some cattle on somewhere in Arkansas, and I cozied up to this old heifer. I'd of froze to death without her. Many's the time I think of that old cow. Tried milking her, but it come out frozen solid. Like a Popsicle."<br /><br /> "It's getting high and deep in here," Shug says. He's the only black man I've ever seen in the region, and then only when the rest of the guys are there. He wears a forest-green porkpie hat with the joker from a deck of cards stuck on the side. He's famously intolerant of Daddy's horeshit, and so tends to up the credibility factor when around.<br /><br /> "I shit you not," Daddy says and sprinkles some salt in the triangular hole in his beer can. "You hop one of those bastards some January and ride her. You'll be pissing ice cubes. I guarangoddamntee you that." They shake their heads, and I can see Daddy considering his next move by pretending to study his dominoes. They're lined up like bricks in a wall, and after he chooses one, he makes a show of lining it up right on the tabletop, then marking it down his score. "They unloaded one old boy stiff as a plank from down off the next car over. He was a old one. Didn't have no business riding trains that old. And when we tipped him down to haul him off- they was four or five of us lifting him- about a dozen of these round fuzzy things rolled out of his pant leg. Big as your thumb, and white." He measures off the right length on his thumb.<br /><br /> "These were the crown pearls, no doubt," Shug says.<br /><br /> Daddy stares seriously into the middle distance, as if the old man in question were standing there himself, waiting for his story to get told properly and witnessing the ignorance that Daddy had to suffer in the processs. "Wasn't no such thing. If you shut up, I can tell your thickheaded self what they was."<br /><br /> "Let him tell it," Cooter says, then lowers his head into a cloud of cigar smoke. Cooter is bothered by the fact that Shug is colored, and takes any chance to scold him, which the other guys tend to ignore. Shug gives me Fig Newtons out of his glove compartment, and I feel evil seeing him scolded for no reason and not saying anything. But I know the rules and so lay down.<br /><br /> "One old boy had a big black skillet in his gear. So we built a fire on the edge of the freight yard. It was a kind of hobo camp already there, some other guys set up all around. Nobody bothered us. This old fella's stretched out behind us stiff as this bench I'm sitting on here."<br /><br /> "The dead one?" I ask, and the men shift around in their chairs, a signal for me to shut up, so I do.<br /><br /> "That's right. And you ain't never gonna guess what happens when they thaw." This is the turning point. Daddy cocks his head at everybody to savor it. The men don't even fake indifference. The domino tiles stop their endless clicking. The cigar smoke might even seem to quit winding around on itself for a minute. Nobody so much as takes a drink. "They pop like firecrackers and let off the biggest stink you ever smelled."<br /><br /> "They was farts?" Cooter finally screams, more high-pitched than is masculine, and at that the men start to laugh. Daddy's Adam's apple googles up and down, and Ben slaps the table, and Shug has to wipe his eyes after a minute. <br /><br /> When everybody settles down, Daddy passes the bottle around again and jumps back to the story of his coming home without even a stab at a segue. "Ain't nothing else to tell. I just walked up through the razor grass to Daddy's old dirt road. And there's the old man, sitting on the porch. Just exactly like I left him the year before. And he looks up at me serious as polio and says "You git the coffee?"<br /><br /> To Mother, such stories showed that Daddy offered steadiness. He always returned to the logging camp at the end of whatever journey, and coming back was something she'd begun to need from a man, badly. He was a rock. Guys he worked with claimed you could set a watch by when he pulled into the parking lot or what time he clicked open his lunch box. When Mother described Daddy's childhood to us, she would sometimes fake horror at how savage it all sounded- boiling the bristles off a live hog and so forth. But really she admired this world, as she admired the scratchy misery of the blues she listened to on her Bessie Smith records."<br /><br /><br /><em>Damn.</em> That's some storytelling! On both sides, I mean. Right away you get a sense of the kind of man Peter Karr was and where the obvious storytelling gift ended up. <br /><br /> The 'Mother' is quite a piece of work- defiant, intellectual, obsessed with Existentialism, known for carrying a copy of Das Kapital in her purse (daddy Karr was a good union man, suffered through many strikes, and (accurately, as I see it) characterized Republicans as 'people who can't eat a decent meal without knowing that somebody else doesn't have anything') and she unravels as the narrative unfurls, deeply complicating her role in the text. <br /><br /> She also attempts some seriously fucked up violence on little Mary and her older sister Lecia ('Lisa', if you don't mind, and you ought to- this little one talks down state troopers while she's driving plastered and several dozen miles over the limit) which seems to come out of nowhere and is absolutely terrifying in the way Karr depicts it. Psychosis is no joke and Karr doesn't flinch from harrowingly articulate detail.<br /><br /> Reading it, I kept thinking of Jeanette Walls's "The Glass Castle". There's so much these two have in common- the abjectness of their fierce, surprisingly bookish, constantly chaotic and deeply apocalyptic childhoods. You've gotten the charismatic, occasionally brilliant, constantly drunken fathers, the equally drunken, rebellious, guilt-ridden, artistically frustrated mothers....the immediacy of their childhoods is all-too-vivid and consistently absorbing and nothing close to solipsistic or sensationalized. <br /><br /> I know that these two books, being huge hits back when they first come out, helped spark the sad kid memoir craze and thus paved the way for wankers like Augusten Burroughs and James Frey, but Karr and Walls are miles beyond those two ass clowns both in literay talent and in brutal, unsentimental honesty and searing emotion.<br /><br /> Somewhere, someday, across a sparklingly white picket fence, Mary Karr and Jeanette Walls are going to meet, strike up a conversation, start comparing battle scars and blurry tall tales, and become instant best friends over martinis and whorls of cigarette smoke...<br /><br /> Pretty much exactly like Sylvia Plath and Anne Sexton used to do back in Boston, back in the day, come to think of it. (Long life! Long life!)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9515401240794059296">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_274262110">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/274262110" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/565363-mark" class="left imgcol" title="mark"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1416705427p2/565363.jpg" alt="mark" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/274262110?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 26, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/565363-mark" class="user" title="mark">mark</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">writers, psych persons, girls, parents</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/565363-mark?shelf=relationships" class="actionLinkLite">relationships</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/565363-mark?shelf=nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/565363-mark?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/565363-mark?shelf=mental-health" class="actionLinkLite">mental-health</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/565363-mark?shelf=biography-memoir" class="actionLinkLite">biography-memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer274262110">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3831848814343466344">The Liar’s Club is an important book and it speaks to me in several ways. 1) Mary Karr, the author, and I are, both of us, teachers, readers, practitioners and aficionados of the craft of writing; and so as such I say with confidence: This is a well-written book. Mary Karr can flat out write and also tell a story well, a derivative of her childhood (which this is the story of) when she used to observe her father, Daddy, sitting amongst friends and telling stories, aka lies, while he played cards</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3831848814343466344">The Liar’s Club is an important book and it speaks to me in several ways. 1) Mary Karr, the author, and I are, both of us, teachers, readers, practitioners and aficionados of the craft of writing; and so as such I say with confidence: This is a well-written book. Mary Karr can flat out write and also tell a story well, a derivative of her childhood (which this is the story of) when she used to observe her father, Daddy, sitting amongst friends and telling stories, aka lies, while he played cards, smoked, and drank whiskey. Karr was seven when she begins, and the first part of the book covers just that year, 1961, in Port Arthur, Texas, which she calls Leechfield – which all of the above warns us readers as to the veracity of this memoir. 2) Karr and I grew up in the same cultural time in America (I am four years older) so of which she speaks – the man/woman/children/work/home thing takes me back and rings true. 3) The second part of the book takes place in Colorado in 1963, when on a family cross-country road trip, the state wound up being her home for a time. It was truly the ‘Wild West’ and was impressive – the people and the land. It so happened that in 1963, I too, discovered Colorado on a cross-country road trip with my parents and was awestruck by the land and people – the red rocks of the Garden of the Gods, and the cowboy chuck-wagon campfire sing-a-longs and horseback riding. It’s possible our trails crossed – on horseback! 4) Mary Karr was sexually, physically, emotionally, and psychologically abused, as well as neglected when she was a child. In, 1996, just after publication of this book and when Karr was 42, I was 46 and began three years of living with, and counseling children, ages 6 – 13, who had been treated very similar to how and what Karr had experienced. Again, the story rings true. But Karr adds something to that story that in no other way can be known; and this goes to her writing style and voice. She tells this tale in a first person narrative, as if the 7/9 year old girl is speaking and yet she uses the language of a sophisticated, erudite adult at times commingled with vulgar hillbilly child speak, and so is able to put into words that which is impossible for a 7/9 year old to do. [I hope that makes sense.] The best example of that is when she tells how she felt, and what it was like, to give her male baby-sitter a blow job. And how at the same time such a thing can be revolting and yet somehow funny. In other words – confusing to a child of nine. (pg. 239 – 247) The writing and experience is stunning. 5) Here is where it all comes together from my perspective at age 62, in 2012. Mary Karr’s book is interpretative (it has to be.) And she does what victims of child abuse and neglect often do – minimize what happened and romanticize their parents. Daddy was not a very good dad. Oh sure, he could have been worse. And Mommy was pretty much awful. Oh sure there were reasons, good reasons, for her behavior (we readers find out at the end.) On page 261 the author, as the author, said this: “(Sure the world breeds monsters, but kindness grows just as wild, elsewise every raped baby would grow up to rape.)” True enough, but only by half. Flip that fact and look at it this way: Almost all (90%+ or -) persons who do do monstrous things had monstrous things done to them. And therein lies the riddle, why some and not all. <br /><br />I think Karr’s interpretation is part of the story, her story, brilliantly told, but it isn’t the whole story – of childhood abuse ‘and’ memoir writing. But she is right when she says in the intro, I think, “… talk about it – and you get better.” This book will help. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3831848814343466344">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_762841818">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/762841818" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7405304-deb-stone" class="left imgcol" title="Deb Stone"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1393833142p2/7405304.jpg" alt="Deb Stone" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/762841818?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 16, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7405304-deb-stone" class="user" title="Deb Stone">Deb Stone</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7405304-deb-stone?shelf=memoir" class="actionLinkLite">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer762841818">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2211073824314222209">Karr's memoir is indeed a gritty accounting of a tough childhood. She recounts the many stories of subpar parenting with little self-pity, and with surprising neutrality, and occasional empathy toward her parents. The reader experiences her shift in focus, attachment, and blame from mom to dad and back to mom again in ways that feel as organic as shifting alliances in life as a child's understanding of his or her parent's failings becomes clear. I appreciated Karr's ability to admit her own unce</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2211073824314222209">Karr's memoir is indeed a gritty accounting of a tough childhood. She recounts the many stories of subpar parenting with little self-pity, and with surprising neutrality, and occasional empathy toward her parents. The reader experiences her shift in focus, attachment, and blame from mom to dad and back to mom again in ways that feel as organic as shifting alliances in life as a child's understanding of his or her parent's failings becomes clear. I appreciated Karr's ability to admit her own uncertainty about parts of the story without detracting from authenticity. She accomplished this in part, by using honest phrases such as "I don't remember..." "I probably...." "They must have been done with..." "The memory turns to smoke right there." By using these markers to say she isn't fully certain about what she's about to tell the reader, she establishes credibility. This builds alliance with the reader through some stark and potentially unbelievable moments. Karr, her sister, and both parents were well described characters that fully inhabited the stories told. I appreciate how the story circled back around to reveal Karr's mother's secrets that had driven her behavior at times, in a way that allowed Karr to empathize with her mother, and in the end, she laments they held the guilt and myths so long they were detrimental. That it might have worked better if they'd been able to see the clear light of truth. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2211073824314222209">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_25755336">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25755336" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/954663-neil" class="left imgcol" title="Neil"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1204300025p2/954663.jpg" alt="Neil" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/25755336?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/954663-neil" class="user" title="Neil">Neil</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer25755336">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2553267448238365406">I don't often read memoirs, and not surprisingly even a well-written one like this didn't do a whole lot for me. One thing I did really like about it were all the times that Karr pointed out the gaps in her memory--the principal whose suit she could remember but whose face she remembered no particular detail about, the portions of a stressful evening that are lost to her. It was a nice way to pull back from a fiction that the memoirist can remember everything and to implicitly raise the question</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2553267448238365406">I don't often read memoirs, and not surprisingly even a well-written one like this didn't do a whole lot for me. One thing I did really like about it were all the times that Karr pointed out the gaps in her memory--the principal whose suit she could remember but whose face she remembered no particular detail about, the portions of a stressful evening that are lost to her. It was a nice way to pull back from a fiction that the memoirist can remember everything and to implicitly raise the question about why certain details stick when others do not.<br /><br />But in the end, it was hard for me to understand the point of the book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2553267448238365406">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_572318871">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/572318871" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5071202-amy-neftzger" class="left imgcol" title="Amy Neftzger"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442454379p2/5071202.jpg" alt="Amy Neftzger" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/572318871?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 17, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5071202-amy-neftzger" class="user" title="Amy Neftzger">Amy Neftzger</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer572318871">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9623156924450393678">I think we all go through hell at one time or another, but Mary Karr's memoir of going through hell as a child of an alcoholic mother is a poignant book about how we deal with those personal hells and survive. The book tells a story of how even when we're loved, we're sometimes lied to ... but we can survive. The prose is often beautiful to the point of poetic, but the content is still real and gut wrenching. The book shows how truth and lies can be both brutal and comforting in the right places</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9623156924450393678">I think we all go through hell at one time or another, but Mary Karr's memoir of going through hell as a child of an alcoholic mother is a poignant book about how we deal with those personal hells and survive. The book tells a story of how even when we're loved, we're sometimes lied to ... but we can survive. The prose is often beautiful to the point of poetic, but the content is still real and gut wrenching. The book shows how truth and lies can be both brutal and comforting in the right places.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/107341268?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 16, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/187563-smetchie" class="user" title="smetchie">smetchie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14241.The_Liars_Club" title="Paperback 9780143035749" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to smetchie by: </span>
      Angela
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/187563-smetchie?shelf=book-club" class="actionLinkLite">book-club</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/187563-smetchie?shelf=depressing" class="actionLinkLite">depressing</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/187563-smetchie?shelf=memoir" class="actionLinkLite">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer107341268">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7512929810498685637">I don't get the fascination with memoirs, as a general rule. So I don't think it's really fair for me to review this book. I'll just say that I appreciated parts of it. For one, the intensely detailed descriptions of her grandmothers leg-rot, subsequent amputation, resulting stump and prosthetic-leg-with-the-little-black-shoe. I <em>do</em> get the fascination with severed appendages, as a general rule.<br /><br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

There have been numerous books about mother and daughter, but none has dealt with this closest of filial relations as d&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText18290244965459098592\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;In this deeply etched and haunting memoir, Vivian Gornick tells the story of her lifelong battle with her mother for independence. There have been numerous books about mother and daughter, but none has dealt with this closest of filial relations as directly or as ruthlessly. Gornick\'s groundbreaking book confronts what Edna O\'Brien has called &amp;quot;the prinicpal crux of female despair&amp;quot;: the unacknowledged Oedipal nature of the mother-daughter bond.\n\nBorn and raised in the Bronx, the daughter of &amp;quot;urban peasants,&amp;quot; Gornick grows up in a household dominated by her intelligent but uneducated mother\'s romantic depression over the early death of her husband. Next door lives Nettie, an attractive widow whose calculating sensuality appeals greatly to Vivian. These women with their opposing models of femininity continue, well into adulthood, to affect Gornick\'s struggle to find herself in love and in work.\n\nAs Gornick walks with her aged mother through the streets of New York, arguing and remembering the past, each wins the reader\'s admiration: the caustic and clear-thinking daughter, for her courage and tenacity in really talking to her mother about the most basic issues of their lives, and the still powerful and intuitively-wise old woman, who again and again proves herself her daughter\'s mother.\n\nUnsparing, deeply courageous, Fierce Attachments is one of the most remarkable documents of family feeling that has been written, a classic that helped start the memoir boom and remains one of the most moving examples of the genre.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"18290244965459098592\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));; return false;\"&gt;...more&lt;\/a&gt;\n\n    &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n      &lt;div Barbara Robinette Moss was the fourth in a family of eight chi&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText15330803254421075602\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;A haunting and triumphant story of a difficult and keenly felt life, Change Me into Zeus\'s Daughter is a remarkable literary memoir of resilience, redemption, and growing up in the South. Barbara Robinette Moss was the fourth in a family of eight children raised in the red-clay hills of Alabama. Their wild-eyed, alcoholic father was a charismatic and irrationally proud man who, when sober, captured his children\'s timid awe, but when (more often) drunk, roused them from bed for severe punishment or bizarre all-night poker games. Their mother was their angel: erudite and stalwart -- her only sin her inability to leave her husband for the sake of the children.  Unlike the rest of her family, Barbara bore the scars of this abuse and neglect on the outside as well as the inside. As a result of childhood malnutrition and a complete lack of medical and dental care, the bones in her face grew abnormally (&amp;quot;like a thin pine tree&amp;quot;), and she ended up with what she calls &amp;quot;a twisted, mummy face.&amp;quot; Barbara\'s memoir brings us deep into not only the world of Southern poverty and alcoholic child abuse but also the consciousness of one who is physically frail and awkward, relating how one girl\'s debilitating sense of her own physical appearance is ultimately saved by her faith in the transformative powers of artistic beauty: painting and writing.  From early on and with little encouragement from the world, Barbara embodied the fiery determination to change her fate and achieve a life defined by beauty. At age seven, she announced to the world that she would become an artist -- and so she did. Nightly, she prayed to become attractive, to be changed into &amp;quot;Zeus\'s daughter,&amp;quot; the goddess of beauty, and when her prayers weren\'t answered, she did it herself, raising the money for years of braces followed by facial surgery. Growing up &amp;quot;so ugly,&amp;quot; she felt the family\'s disgrace all the more acutely, but the result has been a keenly developed appreciation for beauty -- physical and artistic -- the evidence of which can be seen in her writing.  Despite the deprivation, the lingering image from this memoir is not of self-pity but of the incredible bond between these eight siblings: the raucous, childish fun they had together, the making-do, and the total devotion to their desperate mother, who absorbed most of the father\'s blows for them and who plied them with art and poetry in place of balanced meals. Gracefully and intelligently woven in layers of flashback, the persistent strength of Barbara Moss\'s memoir is itself a testament to the nearly lifesaving appreciation for literature that was her mother\'s greatest gift to her children.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"15330803254421075602\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));; return false;\"&gt;...more&lt;\/a&gt;\n\n Both writers, Gail Caldwell and Caroline Knapp, author of Drinking: A Love Story, became best friends, talking about everything from their shared history of a struggle with alcohol, to their relationships with men and colleagues, to their love of books. They walked the woods of New England and rowed on the Charles River, and the miles they logged on land and water became a measure of the interior ground they covered. From disparate backgrounds but with striking emotional similarities, these two private, fiercely self-reliant women created an attachment more profound than either of them could ever have foreseen. \n\nThe friendship helped them define the ordinary moments of life as the ones worth cherishing. Then, several years into this remarkable connection, Knapp was diagnosed with terminal lung cancer.\n\nWith her signature exquisite prose, Caldwell mines the deepest levels of devotion and grief in this moving memoir about treasuring and losing a best friend. Let’s Take the Long Way Home is a celebration of life and of the transformations that come from intimate connection—and it affirms, once again, why Gail Caldwell is recognized as one of our bravest     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8865.Mary_Karr">About Mary Karr</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/8865.Mary_Karr"><img alt="8865" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1264001681p2/8865.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer12833911155407542043"><em>Mary Karr</em> is an American poet, essayist and memoirist. She rose to fame in 1995 with the publication of her bestselling memoir <em>The Liars' Club</em>. She is the Peck Professor of English Literature at Syracuse University.<br /><em>The Liars' Club</em>, published in 1995, was a New York Times bestseller for over a year, and was named one of the year's best books. It delves vividly and often humorously into her deeply t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12833911155407542043"><em>Mary Karr</em> is an American poet, essayist and memoirist. She rose to fame in 1995 with the publication of her bestselling memoir <em>The Liars' Club</em>. She is the Peck Professor of English Literature at Syracuse University.<br /><em>The Liars' Club</em>, published in 1995, was a New York Times bestseller for over a year, and was named one of the year's best books. It delves vividly and often humorously into her deeply troubled childhood, most of which was spent in a gritty, industrial section of Southeast Texas in the 1960s. She was encouraged to write her personal history by her friend, author Tobias Wolff, but has said she only took up the project when her marriage fell apart.<br />She followed the book with another memoir, <em>Cherry</em> (2000), about her late adolescence and early womanhood. A third memoir, <em>Lit</em>, which she says details "my journey from blackbelt sinner and lifelong agnostic to unlikely Catholic," came out in November 2009.<br />Karr thinks of herself first and foremost as a poet. She was a Guggenheim Fellow in poetry in 2005 and has won Pushcart prizes for both her poetry and her essays. Karr has published four volumes of poetry: <em>Abacus</em> (Wesleyan University Press, CT, 1987, in its New Poets series), <em>The Devil's Tour</em> (New Directions NY, 1993, an original TPB), <em>Viper Rum</em> (New Directions NY, 1998, an original TPB), and her new volume <em>Sinners Welcome</em> (HarperCollins, NY 2006). Her poems have appeared in major literary magazines such as Poetry, The New Yorker, and The Atlantic Monthly.<br />She is a controversial figure in the American poetry "establishment," thanks to her Pushcart-award winning essay, "Against Decoration," which was originally published in the quarterly review Parnassus (1991) and later reprinted in <em>Viper Rum</em>. In this essay Karr took a stand in favor of content over poetic style. She argued emotions need to be directly expressed, and clarity should be a watch-word: characters are too obscure, the presented physical world is often "foggy" (that is imprecise), references are "showy" (both non-germane and overused), metaphors over-shadow expected meaning, and techniques of language (polysyllables, archaic words, intricate syntax, "yards of adjectives") only "slow a reader"'s understanding. Karr directly criticized well-known, well-connected, and award-winning poets such as <em>James Merrill</em>, <em>Amy Clampitt</em>, <em>Vijay Seshadri</em>, and <em>Rosanna Warren</em> (daughter of Pulitzer Prize winner <em>Robert Penn Warren</em>). Karr favors controlled elegance to create transcendent poetic meaning out of not-quite-ordinary moments, presenting <em>James Merrill's Charles on Fire</em> as a successful example.<br />While some ornamentations Karr rails against are due to shifting taste, she believes much is due to the revolt against formalism which substituted sheer ornamentation for the discipline of meter. Karr notes <em>Randall Jarrell</em> said much the same thing, albeit more decorously, nearly fifty years ago. Her essay is meant to provide the technical detail to Jarrell's argument. As a result of this essay Karr earned a reputation for being both courageous and combative, a matured version of the BB-gun toting little hellion limned in <em>The Liars' Club</em>.<br />Another essay, "Facing Altars: Poetry and Prayer", was originally published in Poetry (2005). Karr tells of moving from agnostic alcoholic to baptized Catholic of the decidedly "cafeteria" kind, yet one who prays twice daily with loud fervor from her "foxhole". In this essay Karr argues that poetry and prayer arise from the same sources within us.<br />Karr was born January 16, 1955, in Groves, a small town in East Texas located in the Port Arthur region, known for its oil refineries and chemical plants, to J. P. and Charlie Marie (Moore) Karr. In her memoirs, Karr calls the town "Leechfield." Karr's father worked in an oil refinery while her mother was an amateur artist and business owner. Karr's sister, two years her elder, </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12833911155407542043">...more</a>
